\begin{itemize}

\]

Now, consider the "maximal" strategy: Alice always sets S_n = λ n (if possible), and Bazza always sets T_n = n (if possible). As we saw, this is sustainable if λ ≥ 0.5. For λ > 0.5, after each Bazza move, S_n = λ(n-1)+1, and then Alice can set S_{n+1}=λ(n+1) because λ(n+1) - S_n = λ(n+1) - λ(n-1) -1 = 2λ -1 >0. So she can set x_{n+1}=2λ-1. We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy, and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. Since the game is deterministic and perfect information, it's a two-player game with no draws if we consider that infinite play is a draw, but the problem asks for which λ Alice has a winning strategy and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. So Alice wants to make T_{odd} large enough that at the next even turn, Bazza cannot set T_n = n. So she wants to make T_{odd} > n. That would cause Bazza to lose at his turn. So if she can make T_n > n for some odd n, she wins on the next turn. But she also must respect S_n ≤ λ n. So she needs to find a sequence of moves that forces T_n > n at some odd step, regardless of Bazza's choices. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where the players control the "potential" differently.

We need to systematically determine the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and the set for which Bazza has a winning strategy. The problem is likely from an IMO Shortlist or similar, and the answer is something like: Alice wins if λ > 1/√2, Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2, and draw if λ = 1/√2. But we found that for λ=1, Alice wins (we have a winning strategy: set x_1=0, then x_3=3-x_2, which forces T_3>4). However, we need to check if that strategy works for all λ>1? For λ=1, we already saw it works. For λ=0.8, we suspect it's a draw. But what about λ=0.9? Let's test λ=0.9 with Alice's strategy of starting x_1=0. Then Bazza at n=2: he can choose x_2 up to √2≈1.414. If he chooses x_2=1.2, then S_2=1.2, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 2.7 - 1.2 = 1.5, T_3=2+2.25=4.25. Then Bazza at n=4: T_3=4.25 ≤ 4? Actually, 4.25 > 4, so he loses immediately! So if Bazza chooses x_2=1.2, Alice wins on turn 4. But can Bazza choose a different x_2 to avoid that? He can choose x_2 smaller. The function f(x)=x^2+(2.7-x)^2 has minimum at x=1.35, f_min=2*1.35^2-2*1.35*2.7? Let's compute: g(x)=x^2+(2.7-x)^2 = 2x^2 -5.4x +7.29. Vertex at x=1.35, g_min = 2*1.8225 -5.4*1.35+7.29 = 3.645 -7.29+7.29=3.645. So the minimum T_3 is 3.645, which is less than 4. So Bazza can choose x_2=1.35 to make T_3=3.645 < 4. Then he can play x_4=√(4-3.645)=√0.355=0.596, S_4=2.7+0.596=3.296, T_4=4. Then Alice at n=5: S_4=3.296 ≤ 4.5, so x_5=4.5-3.296=1.204, T_5=4+1.450=5.450, then Bazza at n=6: T_5=5.450 ≤ 6, so x_6=√(6-5.450)=√0.55=0.742, S_6=4.5+0.742=5.242, T_6=6. Then Alice at n=7: S_6=5.242 ≤ 6.3, so x_7=6.3-5.242=1.058, T_7=6+1.119=7.119, then Bazza at n=8: T_7=7.119 ≤ 8, so x_8=√(8-7.119)=√0.881=0.939, S_8=6.3+0.939=7.239, T_8=8. Then Alice at n=9: S_8=7.239 ≤ 8.1, x_9=8.1-7.239=0.861, T_9=8+0.741=8.741, then Bazza at n=10: T_9=8.741 ≤ 10, x_10=√(10-8.741)=√1.259=1.122, S_10=8.1+1.122=9.222, T_10=10. So again continuation. So for λ=0.9, the maximal strategy yields infinite play if Bazza chooses x_2=1.35. So Alice's strategy of x_1=0 does not guarantee a win because Bazza can choose x_2 to keep T_3 ≤ 4. So she needs a different strategy.

Now, we need to find the threshold where Alice can force a win. Let's analyze the game more abstractly.

Define the game state after each turn. Let's denote the cumulative sums at the start of a turn (before the player's move). For odd n, Alice moves; for even n, Bazza moves. The game ends when a player cannot move.

We can think of it as a game where Alice controls the sum S_n and Bazza controls the sum of squares T_n. The constraints are linear in S and quadratic in T. The game is reminiscent of a "boundary" game where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the boundary.

A standard technique for such problems is to use "strategy stealing" or "invariant" to determine the outcome. Possibly we can transform the game into a game about sequences with a "potential" that is the "difference" between the sum and the sum of squares bound.

Observation: The constraints involve S_n ≤ λ n and T_n ≤ n. Also, by Cauchy-Schwarz, T_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if S_n is large, T_n is at least something. Conversely, if T_n is small, S_n is at most sqrt(n T_n). So there is a relationship.

Suppose we want to see if Bazza can force a win for λ < something. For Bazza to win, he needs to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. He can control S_n by choosing x_n at even n. He must keep T_n ≤ n. So his ability to increase S_n is limited by the sum of squares. He wants to make S_n as large as possible relative to λ n. Since he controls x_n, he can set x_n = sqrt(n - T_{n-1}) to achieve T_n = n. Then S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). This is the maximum possible S_n given the past. So if Bazza always sets T_n = n (if possible), then S_n increases by sqrt(1) = 1 at each even step? Actually, if T_{n-1}=n-1 (since previous even turn set it to n-1), then sqrt(n - (n-1)) = 1, so S_n = S_{n-1} + 1. So S_n increases by 1 at each even step. For odd n, Alice can also increase S_n by at most λ n - S_{n-1}. If she always sets S_n = λ n, then S_n increases by λ at odd steps? Let's see: At odd n, S_{n-1} is from previous even step. If Bazza always sets T_n = n, then S_{even} = λ (even-1) + 1? Not exactly. Let's derive recurrence assuming both play to maximize their own sums.

Assume Bazza always sets T_n = n (if possible). Then at even n, T_n = n. At odd n, Alice sets S_n = λ n (if possible). Then after Alice's odd move, S_n = λ n, and T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Since Bazza sets T_{n-1} = n-1 (since n-1 is even), we have T_n = n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Then Bazza at next even n+1 must choose x_{n+1} such that T_{n+1} ≤ n+1. He can set T_{n+1} = n+1 if possible, i.e., if (n+1) - T_n ≥ 0. That is, (n+1) - (n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2) ≥ 0 => 2 - (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 ≥ 0 => |λ n - S_{n-1}| ≤ √2. So Bazza can maintain T_{n+1}=n+1 if the increase in S at odd n is at most √2. Since Alice can choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} to be as large as possible, she might try to make it > √2 to cause Bazza to lose. But she must also respect S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. So she can set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. The condition for Bazza to survive is that λ n - S_{n-1} ≤ √2. If she can make it > √2, then T_n > n+1? Wait, T_n = n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. If λ n - S_{n-1} > √2, then (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > 2, so T_n > n-1+2 = n+1, so at the start of Bazza's next turn (even n+1), T_n > n+1, so Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So she wants to make the increase at an odd turn exceed √2. But she can only do that if S_{n-1} is sufficiently small. Since S_{n-1} is the sum after the previous even turn, which is controlled by Bazza. Bazza can try to make S_{n-1} as large as possible to limit the increase.

So the game becomes: at each even step, Bazza can choose x_n to increase S_n, but he must keep T_n ≤ n. He can also choose to set T_n smaller than n to allow later larger increases? Actually, he wants to make S_{odd} as large as possible so that Alice cannot make a large increase. But if he makes S_{odd} too large, Alice might be forced to set x_n=0 (if S_{odd} > λ n) and lose? No, Alice loses if S_{odd} > λ n. But Bazza wants to cause that. So Bazza's goal is to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. He can try to push S_n high by setting x_n large. But his constraint is T_n ≤ n. So his ability to increase S_n is limited by the sum of squares.

Thus the game is like a race: Alice wants to make the increase at some odd turn exceed √2, while Bazza wants to make the increase at some even turn exceed λ? Actually, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. Let's formalize.

Let’s define a sequence of moves. We can think of the game as a sequence of "rounds" where Alice moves on odd n and Bazza on even n. We can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for the existence of an infinite play. This is similar to a "two-player" version of the inequality (S_n)^2 ≤ n T_n, and the game is to force violation of either S_n ≤ λ n or T_n ≤ n. The player who can force the opponent's constraint to be violated wins.

I recall a known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where on odd turns Alice chooses x_n such that sum ≤ λ n, on even turns Bazza chooses x_n such that sum of squares ≤ n. Determine λ for which each has a winning strategy." The answer is often: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and draw if λ = 1. But we saw that for λ=1, Alice can win by setting x_1=0, x_3=3-x_2. However, we also saw that Bazza can avoid losing by playing x_2=1, then the game continues indefinitely. So is that a winning strategy for Alice? Let's analyze λ=1 carefully.

For λ=1, Alice's strategy: x_1=0. Then Bazza at n=2: he must choose x_2 such that T_2 = x_2^2 ≤ 2. He can choose x_2 = √2 ≈1.414, but then S_2=1.414, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 3 - S_2 = 1.586, T_3 = 2 + (1.586)^2 ≈ 2+2.516=4.516. Then at n=4, Bazza must satisfy T_4 ≤ 4. But T_3=4.516 > 4, so he cannot move and loses. So if Bazza chooses x_2=√2, Alice wins. But can Bazza choose a different x_2 to avoid that? He can choose x_2 = 1, then S_2=1, T_2=1. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 3 - 1 = 2, T_3=1+4=5. Then at n=4, T_3=5>4, so Bazza loses. So if she sets x_3=2, she wins. But wait, can she set x_3=2? S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = 1+2=3, which is ≤ λ*3=3, so yes. So again she wins. What if Bazza chooses x_2=0? Then S_2=0, T_2=0. Then Alice sets x_3=3, T_3=9, and Bazza loses at n=4. So no matter what x_2 Bazza chooses, Alice can set x_3 = 3 - x_2 (since λ*3=3), and then T_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2. For x_2 ∈ [0,√2], the minimum of T_3 is at x_2=1.5, but x_2 ≤ √2≈1.414, so the minimum is at x_2=1.414, giving T_3=2+ (1.586)^2=2+2.516=4.516 > 4. So T_3 > 4 always. So Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice has a winning strategy for λ=1. So λ=1 is not a draw. But our earlier analysis for λ=1/√2 gave a draw. So the threshold seems to be λ0 = 1? But we also have λ=0.8, where we saw that with the maximal strategy, the game can continue indefinitely. But does Alice have a winning strategy for λ=0.8? Possibly she could use a different first move to force a win. Let's test λ=0.8 with Alice's strategy of setting x_1=0. We saw that Bazza can choose x_2=1.2 to make T_3=3.645 < 4, so he survives. But maybe Alice can choose a different x_1 that forces a win. For instance, she could set x_1=0.8? Then as we saw, Bazza can choose x_2=1.166? That gave continuation. But maybe she could set x_1=0.5? That gave Bazza can choose x_2=1.323 to survive. So it seems that for λ=0.8, Bazza can always respond to keep the game going. Could Alice try to set x_1 such that T_1 is large enough that at the next even turn, she can make a big increase? For λ=0.8, suppose she sets x_1=0.8, T_1=0.64. Then Bazza at n=2 can choose x_2=√(2-0.64)=1.166, S_2=1.966, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 2.4 - 1.966 = 0.434, T_3=2+0.188=2.188. That's small. But she could also choose a smaller x_3? That wouldn't help. Alternatively, she could choose a larger x_1? If she sets x_1=0.8, T_1=0.64, then Bazza can choose x_2=0, then S_2=0.8, T_2=0.64, Alice at n=3: she can set x_3=2.4-0.8=1.6, T_3=0.64+2.56=3.2 < 4, then Bazza at n=4: T_3=3.2 ≤4, so he can set x_4=√(4-3.2)=√0.8=0.894, S_4=2.4+0.894=3.294, T_4=4. Then Alice at n=5: S_4=3.294 ≤4.0, so x_5=0.706, T_5=4+0.498=4.498, then Bazza at n=6: T_5=4.498 ≤6, so x_6=√(6-4.498)=√1.502=1.226, S_6=4.0+1.226=5.226, T_6=6. Then Alice at n=7: S_6=5.226 ≤5.6, x_7=0.374, T_7=6+0.140=6.14, Bazza at n=8: T_7=6.14 ≤8, x_8=√(8-6.14)=√1.86=1.364, S_8=5.6+1.364=6.964, T_8=8. So game continues. So no win.

Maybe Alice can aim to make T_n exceed n at some later odd turn by choosing a larger x at an odd turn than λ n - S_{n-1}? That's not allowed. So the only way to win is to make T_{odd} > (odd+1) maybe? Actually, if after Alice's move at odd n, T_n > n+1, then Bazza loses at his next turn. So she wants to achieve T_n > n+1 at some odd n. Since T_n = T_{n-1} + x_n^2, and x_n ≤ λ n - S_{n-1}. So the maximum possible T_n given state (S_{n-1}, T_{n-1}) is T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can achieve T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 if she sets x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. So her ability to push T_n up depends on the gap between S_{n-1} and λ n. Since S_{n-1} is from previous even turn, which is controlled by Bazza. Bazza can try to keep S_{n-1} large to limit the increase. But if Bazza makes S_{n-1} too large, Alice might lose because S_{n-1} > λ n? Actually, if at the start of her turn (odd n), we have S_{n-1} > λ n, then she cannot move and loses immediately. So Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n at some odd n. So he wants to push S_n high. But his constraint is T_n ≤ n. So he can increase S_n by at most sqrt(n - T_{n-1}) at each even step.

Thus the game is essentially about two sequences: S_n and T_n, with the constraints that for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n; for even n, T_n ≤ n. And the players can control the increments.

We need to find the threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; and for λ = λ0, draw (infinite play). From our examples, λ=1 gives Alice win; λ=0.5 gives Bazza win; λ=0.7071 (1/√2) gave draw? But we need to verify if λ=1/√2 is indeed a draw. Thus λ0 = 1/√2 might be the threshold. But we saw that λ=1 > 0.7071 gives Alice win, and λ=0.5 < 0.7071 gives Bazza win. Since both sides are nonnegative, we can square and solve.

Let’s denote:

u2 = √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2). Square: u2^2 = 2 - (3λ - √2)^2 = 2 - (9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 2) = 2 - 9λ^2 + 6√2 λ - 2 = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

So u2^2 = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

The equation u2 = λ - √(1-λ^2). Square both sides:

(λ - √(1-λ^2))^2 = λ^2 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) + 1 - λ^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

Thus we have:

1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

Rearrange:

1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

=> 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Square again to eliminate the square root. But first note that we need λ ≥ 1/√2 for the RHS to be positive? Actually, we need the RHS to be nonnegative because LHS is nonnegative. So λ must satisfy 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2 ≥ 0.

Now, square:

(2λ√(1-λ^2))^2 = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

=> 4λ^2 (1-λ^2) = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

=> 4λ^2 - 4λ^4 = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

This is a quartic equation. We can try to solve for λ.

Let’s set x = λ. Then:

4x^2 - 4x^4 = (1 - 6√2 x + 9x^2)^2.

Take square root? Actually, we can consider that the equation might have a nice solution. Notice that λ=1/√2? Let's test: λ=1/√2≈0.7071. Then u2 = √(2 - (2.1213-1.4142)^2)=√(2 - (0.7071)^2)=√(2-0.5)=√1.5=1.2247. u0 = λ - √(1-λ^2)=0.7071 - √(1-0.5)=0.7071-0.7071=0. So u2 > u0. So not critical. λ=0.92? We can square the equation u2 = λ - √(1-λ^2). But note that we might also consider the possibility that the critical λ is where the orbit from u2 goes exactly to the boundary 2λ-√2. That might give a different equation. However, our numerical exploration suggests that the transition occurs when u2 = u0. So let's solve for λ.

We have:

√(6√2 λ - 9λ^2) = λ - √(1-λ^2). (since u2 = √(6√2 λ - 9λ^2))

Square both sides:

6√2 λ - 9λ^2 = (λ - √(1-λ^2))^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

=> 6√2 λ - 9λ^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

=> 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Now, square again:

4λ^2 (1-λ^2) = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

We can expand and simplify. Let’s denote:

A = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Then A^2 = 1 + 72λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ + 18λ^2 - 108√2 λ^3? Let's do systematically.

Compute A = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

A^2 = (1)^2 + (-6√2 λ)^2 + (9λ^2)^2 + 2*1*(-6√2 λ) + 2*1*9λ^2 + 2*(-6√2 λ)*(9λ^2)
= 1 + 72λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ + 18λ^2 - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + (72+18)λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Now, the left side: 4λ^2(1-λ^2) = 4λ^2 - 4λ^4.

Thus the equation is:

4λ^2 - 4λ^4 = 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Bring all terms to one side:

0 = 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3 - 4λ^2 + 4λ^4
= 1 + (90-4)λ^2 + (81+4)λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + 86λ^2 + 85λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Thus:

85λ^4 - 108√2 λ^3 + 86λ^2 - 12√2 λ + 1 = 0.

This is a quartic in λ. We need to find a positive root. This might factor as (aλ^2 + bλ + c)^2? Possibly it's a perfect square. Let's check if it can be written as (αλ^2 + βλ + γ)^2. Expanding: (αλ^2 + βλ + γ)^2 = α^2λ^4 + 2αβλ^3 + (2αγ + β^2)λ^2 + 2βγλ + γ^2. We need α^2 = 85, 2αβ = -108√2, 2αγ + β^2 = 86, 2βγ = -12√2, γ^2 = 1. So γ = ±1. If γ=1, then 2βγ = 2β = -12√2 => β = -6√2. Then 2αβ = 2α(-6√2) = -12√2 α = -108√2 => α = 9. Then α^2 = 81, not 85. So not a perfect square with integer α. If γ=-1, then 2β(-1) = -2β = -12√2 => β = 6√2. Then 2αβ = 2α(6√2)=12√2α = -108√2 => α = -9. Then α^2 =81, still not 85. So no perfect square.

So Alice wants to make T_{odd} large enough that at the next even turn, Bazza cannot set T_n = n. So she wants to make T_{odd} > n. That would cause Bazza to lose at his turn. So she needs to find a sequence of moves that forces T_n > n at some odd step, regardless of Bazza's choices. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where the players control the "potential" differently.

We need to systematically determine the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and the set for which Bazza has a winning strategy. The problem is likely from an IMO Shortlist or similar, and the answer is something like: Alice wins if λ > 1/√2, Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2, and draw if λ = 1/√2. But we found that for λ=1, Alice wins (we have a winning strategy: set x_1=0, then x_3=3-x_2, which forces T_3>4). However, we need to check if that strategy works for all λ>1? For λ=1, we already saw it works. For λ=0.8, we suspect it's a draw. But what about λ=0.9? Let's test λ=0.9 with Alice's strategy of starting x_1=0. Then Bazza at n=2: he can choose x_2 up to √2≈1.414. If he chooses x_2=1.2, then S_2=1.2, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 2.7 - 1.2 = 1.5, T_3=2+2.25=4.25. Then Bazza at n=4: T_3=4.25 ≤ 4? Actually, 4.25 > 4, so he loses immediately! So if Bazza chooses x_2=1.2, Alice wins on turn 4. But can Bazza choose a different x_2 to avoid that? He can choose x_2 smaller. The function f(x)=x^2+(2.7-x)^2 has minimum at x=1.35, f_min=2*1.35^2-2*1.35*2.7? Let's compute: g(x)=x^2+(2.7-x)^2 = 2x^2 -5.4x +7.29. Vertex at x=1.35, g_min = 2*1.8225 -5.4*1.35+7.29 = 3.645 -7.29+7.29=3.645. So the minimum T_3 is 3.645, which is less than 4. So Bazza can choose x_2=1.35 to make T_3=3.645 < 4. Then he can play x_4=√(4-3.645)=√0.355=0.596, S_4=2.7+0.596=3.296, T_4=4. Then Alice at n=5: S_4=3.296 ≤ 4.5, so x_5=4.5-3.296=1.204, T_5=4+1.450=5.450, then Bazza at n=6: T_5=5.450 ≤ 6, so x_6=√(6-5.450)=√0.55=0.742, S_6=4.5+0.742=5.242, T_6=6. Then Alice at n=7: S_6=5.242 ≤ 6.3, so x_7=6.3-5.242=1.058, T_7=6+1.119=7.119, then Bazza at n=8: T_7=7.119 ≤ 8, so x_8=√(8-7.119)=√0.881=0.939, S_8=6.3+0.939=7.239, T_8=8. Then Alice at n=9: S_8=7.239 ≤ 8.1, x_9=8.1-7.239=0.861, T_9=8+0.741=8.741, then Bazza at n=10: T_9=8.741 ≤ 10, x_10=√(10-8.741)=√1.259=1.122, S_10=8.1+1.122=9.222, T_10=10. So again continuation. So for λ=0.9, the maximal strategy yields infinite play if Bazza chooses x_2=1.35. So Alice's strategy of x_1=0 does not guarantee a win because Bazza can choose x_2 to keep T_3 ≤ 4. So she needs a different strategy.

Now, we need to find the threshold where Alice can force a win. Let's analyze the game more abstractly.

Define the game state after each turn. Let's denote the cumulative sums at the start of a turn (before the player's move). For odd n, Alice moves; for even n, Bazza moves. The game ends when a player cannot move.

We can think of it as a game where Alice controls the sum S_n and Bazza controls the sum of squares T_n. The constraints are linear in S and quadratic in T. The game is reminiscent of a "boundary" game where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the boundary.

A standard technique for such problems is to use "strategy stealing" or "invariant" to determine the outcome. Possibly we can transform the game into a game about sequences with a "potential" that is the "difference" between the sum and the sum of squares bound.

Observation: The constraints involve S_n ≤ λ n and T_n ≤ n. Also, by Cauchy-Schwarz, T_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if S_n is large, T_n is at least something. Conversely, if T_n is small, S_n is at most sqrt(n T_n). So there is a relationship.

Suppose we want to see if Bazza can force a win for λ < something. For Bazza to win, he needs to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. He can control S_n by choosing x_n at even n. He must keep T_n ≤ n. So his ability to increase S_n is limited by the sum of squares. He wants to make S_n as large as possible relative to λ n. Since he controls x_n, he can set x_n = sqrt(n - T_{n-1}) to achieve T_n = n. Then S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). This is the maximum possible S_n given the past. So if Bazza always sets T_n = n (if possible), then S_n increases by sqrt(1) = 1 at each even step? Actually, if T_{n-1}=n-1 (since previous even turn set it to n-1), then sqrt(n - (n-1)) = 1, so S_n = S_{n-1} + 1. So S_n increases by 1 at each even step. For odd n, Alice can also increase S_n by at most λ n - S_{n-1}. If she always sets S_n = λ n, then S_n increases by λ at odd steps? Let's see: At odd n, S_{n-1} is from previous even step. If Bazza always sets T_n = n, then S_{even} = λ (even-1) + 1? Not exactly. Let's derive recurrence assuming both play to maximize their own sums.

Assume Bazza always sets T_n = n (if possible). Then at even n, T_n = n. At odd n, Alice sets S_n = λ n (if possible). Then after Alice's odd move, S_n = λ n, and T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Since Bazza sets T_{n-1} = n-1 (since n-1 is even), we have T_n = n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Then Bazza at next even n+1 must choose x_{n+1} such that T_{n+1} ≤ n+1. He can set T_{n+1} = n+1 if possible, i.e., if (n+1) - T_n ≥ 0. That is, (n+1) - (n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2) ≥ 0 => 2 - (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 ≥ 0 => |λ n - S_{n-1}| ≤ √2. So Bazza can maintain T_{n+1}=n+1 if the increase in S at odd n is at most √2. Since Alice can choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} to be as large as possible, she might try to make it > √2 to cause Bazza to lose. But she must also respect S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. So she can set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. The condition for Bazza to survive is that λ n - S_{n-1} ≤ √2. If she can make it > √2, then T_n > n+1? Wait, T_n = n-1 + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. If λ n - S_{n-1} > √2, then (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > 2, so T_n > n-1+2 = n+1, so at the start of Bazza's next turn (even n+1), T_n > n+1, so Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So she wants to make the increase at an odd turn exceed √2. But she can only do that if S_{n-1} is sufficiently small. Since S_{n-1} is the sum after the previous even turn, which is controlled by Bazza. Bazza can try to make S_{n-1} as large as possible to limit the increase.

So the game becomes: at each even step, Bazza can choose x_n to increase S_n, but he must keep T_n ≤ n. He can also choose to set T_n smaller than n to allow later larger increases? Actually, he wants to make S_{odd} as large as possible so that Alice cannot make a large increase. But if he makes S_{odd} too large, Alice might be forced to set x_n=0 (if S_{odd} > λ n) and lose? No, Alice loses if S_{odd} > λ n. But Bazza wants to cause that. So Bazza's goal is to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. He can try to push S_n high by setting x_n large. But his constraint is T_n ≤ n. So his ability to increase S_n is limited by the sum of squares.

Thus the game is like a race: Alice wants to make the increase at some odd turn exceed √2, while Bazza wants to make the increase at some even turn exceed λ? Actually, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. Let's formalize.

Let’s define a sequence of moves. We can think of the game as a sequence of "rounds" where Alice moves on odd n and Bazza on even n. We can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for the existence of an infinite play. This is similar to a "two-player" version of the inequality (S_n)^2 ≤ n T_n, and the game is to force violation of either S_n ≤ λ n or T_n ≤ n. The player who can force the opponent's constraint to be violated wins.

I recall a known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where on odd turns Alice chooses x_n such that sum ≤ λ n, on even turns Bazza chooses x_n such that sum of squares ≤ n. Determine λ for which each has a winning strategy." The answer is often: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and draw if λ = 1. But we saw that for λ=1, Alice can win by setting x_1=0, x_3=3-x_2. However, we also saw that Bazza can avoid losing by playing x_2=1, then the game continues indefinitely. So is that a winning strategy for Alice? Let's analyze λ=1 carefully.

For λ=1, Alice's strategy: x_1=0. Then Bazza at n=2: he must choose x_2 such that T_2 = x_2^2 ≤ 2. He can choose x_2 = √2 ≈1.414, but then S_2=1.414, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 3 - S_2 = 1.586, T_3 = 2 + (1.586)^2 ≈ 2+2.516=4.516. Then at n=4, Bazza must satisfy T_4 ≤ 4. But T_3=4.516 > 4, so he cannot move and loses. So if Bazza chooses x_2=√2, Alice wins. But can Bazza choose a different x_2 to avoid that? He can choose x_2 = 1, then S_2=1, T_2=1. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 3 - 1 = 2, T_3=1+4=5. Then at n=4, T_3=5>4, so Bazza loses. So if she sets x_3=2, she wins. But wait, can she set x_3=2? S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = 1+2=3, which is ≤ λ*3=3, so yes. So again she wins. What if Bazza chooses x_2=0? Then S_2=0, T_2=0. Then Alice sets x_3=3, T_3=9, and Bazza loses at n=4. So no matter what x_2 Bazza chooses, Alice can set x_3 = 3 - x_2 (since λ*3=3), and then T_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2. For x_2 ∈ [0,√2], the minimum of T_3 is at x_2=1.5, but x_2 ≤ √2≈1.414, so the minimum is at x_2=1.414, giving T_3=2+ (1.586)^2=2+2.516=4.516 > 4. So T_3 > 4 always. So Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice has a winning strategy for λ=1. So λ=1 is not a draw. But our earlier analysis for λ=1/√2 gave a draw. So the threshold seems to be λ0 = 1? But we also have λ=0.8, where we saw that with the maximal strategy, the game can continue indefinitely. But does Alice have a winning strategy for λ=0.8? Possibly she could use a different first move to force a win. Let's test λ=0.8 with Alice's strategy of setting x_1=0. We saw that Bazza can choose x_2=1.2 to make T_3=3.645 < 4, so he survives. But maybe Alice can choose a different x_1 that forces a win. For instance, she could set x_1=0.8? Then as we saw, Bazza can choose x_2=1.166? That gave continuation. But maybe she could set x_1=0.5? That gave Bazza can choose x_2=1.323 to survive. So it seems that for λ=0.8, Bazza can always respond to keep the game going. Could Alice try to set x_1 such that T_1 is large enough that at the next even turn, she can make a big increase? For λ=0.8, suppose she sets x_1=0.8, T_1=0.64. Then Bazza at n=2 can choose x_2=√(2-0.64)=1.166, S_2=1.966, T_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 2.4 - 1.966 = 0.434, T_3=2+0.188=2.188. That's small. But she could also choose a smaller x_3? That wouldn't help. Alternatively, she could choose a larger x_1? If she sets x_1=0.8, T_1=0.64, then Bazza can choose x_2=0, then S_2=0.8, T_2=0.64, Alice at n=3: she can set x_3=2.4-0.8=1.6, T_3=0.64+2.56=3.2 < 4, then Bazza at n=4: T_3=3.2 ≤4, so he can set x_4=√(4-3.2)=√0.8=0.894, S_4=2.4+0.894=3.294, T_4=4. Then Alice at n=5: S_4=3.294 ≤4.0, so x_5=0.706, T_5=4+0.498=4.498, then Bazza at n=6: T_5=4.498 ≤6, so x_6=√(6-4.498)=√1.502=1.226, S_6=4.0+1.226=5.226, T_6=6. Maybe Alice can aim to make T_n exceed n at some later odd turn by choosing a larger x at an odd turn than λ n - S_{n-1}? That's not allowed. So the only way to win is to make T_{odd} > (odd+1) maybe? Actually, if after Alice's move at odd n, T_n > n+1, then Bazza loses at his next turn. So she wants to achieve T_n > n+1 at some odd n. Since T_n = T_{n-1} + x_n^2, and x_n ≤ λ n - S_{n-1}. So the maximum possible T_n given state (S_{n-1}, T_{n-1}) is T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can achieve T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 if she sets x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. So her ability to push T_n up depends on the gap between S_{n-1} and λ n. Since S_{n-1} is from previous even turn, which is controlled by Bazza. Bazza can try to keep S_{n-1} large to limit the increase. But if Bazza makes S_{n-1} too large, Alice might lose because S_{n-1} > λ n? Actually, if at the start of her turn (odd n), we have S_{n-1} > λ n, then she cannot move and loses immediately. So Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n at some odd n. So he wants to push S_n high. But his constraint is T_n ≤ n. So he can increase S_n by at most sqrt(n - T_{n-1}) at each even step.

Thus the game is essentially about two sequences: S_n and T_n, with the constraints that for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n; for even n, T_n ≤ n. And the players can control the increments.

We need to find the threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; and for λ = λ0, draw (infinite play). From our examples, λ=1 gives Alice win; λ=0.5 gives Bazza win; λ=0.7071 (1/√2) gave draw? But we need to verify if λ=1/√2 is indeed a draw. Thus λ0 = 1/√2 might be the threshold. But we saw that λ=1 > 0.7071 gives Alice win, and λ=0.5 < 0.7071 gives Bazza win. This is equivalent to finding λ such that the orbit of the map f(u) = √(2 - (2λ - u)^2) starting from u1 = √2 eventually yields |2λ - u_k| > √2. This is a dynamical system. We can try to find the threshold λ0 where the orbit converges to a fixed point that is exactly at the boundary? Actually, the threshold should be the λ where the fixed point L satisfies 2λ - L = √2? Or maybe where the orbit is bounded and never escapes. The condition for the orbit to be bounded is that the map has a real fixed point and the initial condition is in the basin of attraction. The map f(u) = √(2 - (2λ - u)^2). This is defined for u in [2λ - √2, 2λ + √2]. The range of f is [0, √2]. So the orbit after the first step lies in [0, √2]. We need to find when the orbit is contained in [0, √2] and never leads to |2λ - u| > √2. The fixed points satisfy u = √(2 - (2λ - u)^2). Squaring: u^2 = 2 - (2λ - u)^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λu - u^2 => 2u^2 - 4λu + 4λ^2 - 2 = 0 => u^2 - 2λu + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0. So fixed points exist if discriminant: 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4 - 4λ^2 = 4(1 - λ^2) ≥ 0 => λ ≤ 1. So for λ ≤ 1, there are two fixed points. For λ > 1, no fixed points. The fixed points are u = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). Since u is in [0, √2], we need to consider the positive ones. For λ ≤ 1, both fixed points are real. The smaller one: u = λ - √(1 - λ^2). Since both sides are nonnegative, we can square and solve.

Let’s denote:

u2 = √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2). Square: u2^2 = 2 - (3λ - √2)^2 = 2 - (9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 2) = 2 - 9λ^2 + 6√2 λ - 2 = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

So u2^2 = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

The equation u2 = λ - √(1-λ^2). Square both sides:

(λ - √(1-λ^2))^2 = λ^2 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) + 1 - λ^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

Thus we have:

1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

Rearrange:

1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 6√2 λ - 9λ^2.

=> 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Square again to eliminate the square root. But first note that we need λ ≥ 1/√2 for the RHS to be positive? Actually, we need the RHS to be nonnegative because LHS is nonnegative. So λ must satisfy 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2 ≥ 0.

Now, square:

(2λ√(1-λ^2))^2 = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

=> 4λ^2 (1-λ^2) = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

=> 4λ^2 - 4λ^4 = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

This is a quartic equation. We can try to solve for λ.

Let’s set x = λ. Then:

4x^2 - 4x^4 = (1 - 6√2 x + 9x^2)^2.

Take square root? Actually, we can consider that the equation might have a nice solution. Notice that λ=1/√2? Let's test: λ=1/√2≈0.7071. Square both sides:

6√2 λ - 9λ^2 = (λ - √(1-λ^2))^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

=> 6√2 λ - 9λ^2 = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2).

=> 2λ√(1-λ^2) = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Now, square again:

4λ^2 (1-λ^2) = (1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2)^2.

We can expand and simplify. Let’s denote:

A = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

Then A^2 = 1 + 72λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ + 18λ^2 - 108√2 λ^3? Let's do systematically.

Compute A = 1 - 6√2 λ + 9λ^2.

A^2 = (1)^2 + (-6√2 λ)^2 + (9λ^2)^2 + 2*1*(-6√2 λ) + 2*1*9λ^2 + 2*(-6√2 λ)*(9λ^2)
= 1 + 72λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ + 18λ^2 - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + (72+18)λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Now, the left side: 4λ^2(1-λ^2) = 4λ^2 - 4λ^4.

Thus the equation is:

4λ^2 - 4λ^4 = 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Bring all terms to one side:

0 = 1 + 90λ^2 + 81λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3 - 4λ^2 + 4λ^4
= 1 + (90-4)λ^2 + (81+4)λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3
= 1 + 86λ^2 + 85λ^4 - 12√2 λ - 108√2 λ^3.

Thus:

85λ^4 - 108√2 λ^3 + 86λ^2 - 12√2 λ + 1 = 0.

This is a quartic in λ. We need to find a positive root. This might factor as (aλ^2 + bλ + c)^2? Possibly it's a perfect square. Let's check if it can be written as (αλ^2 + βλ + γ)^2. Expanding: (αλ^2 + βλ + γ)^2 = α^2λ^4 + 2αβλ^3 + (2αγ + β^2)λ^2 + 2βγλ + γ^2. We need α^2 = 85, 2αβ = -108√2, 2αγ + β^2 = 86, 2βγ = -12√2, γ^2 = 1. So γ = ±1. If γ=1, then 2βγ = 2β = -12√2 => β = -6√2. Then 2αβ = 2α(-6√2) = -12√2 α = -108√2 => α = 9. Then α^2 = 81, not 85. So not a perfect square with integer α. If γ=-1, then 2β(-1) = -2β = -12√2 => β = 6√2. Then 2αβ = 2α(6√2)=12√2α = -108√2 => α = -9. Then α^2 =81, still not 85. So no perfect square.

Square: 6√2 sin φ - 9 sin^2 φ = (sin φ - cos φ)^2 = sin^2 φ - 2 sin φ cos φ + cos^2 φ = 1 - 2 sin φ cos φ.

=> 2 sin φ cos φ = 1 - 6√2 sin φ + 9 sin^2 φ.

=> sin 2φ = 1 - 6√2 sin φ + 9 sin^2 φ.
